\begin{itemize}

\]
\end{itemize}

</self_evaluation>
<score>S</score>

where S is exactly one of 0, 0.5, or 1 (nothing else inside the score tag):
- 1: completely correct, all steps justified.
- 0.5: generally correct, but minor gaps or omissions remain.
- 0: fatal errors, severe omissions, or does not solve the problem.<｜Assistant｜><think>

We need to analyze the game more systematically. Define the state after each turn. The game is infinite horizon; we need to consider if a player can force a win. This is reminiscent of a "control" game where each player has a budget. We can think in terms of "potential" functions.

So Q2 ≥ 1.125. Then Q2 + (3.6-1.5)^2 = 1.125 + 2.1^2 = 1.125+4.41=5.535 >4. If S2=1.8, Q2 ≥ 1.8^2/2 = 3.24/2=1.62, then Q2 + (3.6-1.8)^2 = 1.62 + 1.8^2 = 1.62+3.24=4.86 >4. So it seems that for any feasible (S,Q), Q + (3.6 - S)^2 is >4. But we need to check if there is a combination that makes it ≤4. The minimum of Q + (3.6 - S)^2 over the feasible region might be >4. Let's find the minimum of Q + (3.6 - S)^2 subject to 0 ≤ S ≤ 2, 0 ≤ Q ≤ 2, S^2 ≤ 2Q. Since Q ≥ S^2/2, the objective is at least S^2/2 + (3.6 - S)^2. Let f(S) = S^2/2 + (3.6 - S)^2 = 0.5 S^2 + (3.6^2 - 7.2 S + S^2) = 1.5 S^2 - 7.2 S + 12.96. This is a quadratic in S. Its derivative: 3S - 7.2 =0 => S=2.4. But S is at most 2, so the minimum on [0,2] occurs at the endpoint S=2 (since the parabola opens upward, the vertex at S=2.4 is to the right of the interval, so the function is decreasing on [0,2.4], so minimum at S=2). f(2)=1.5*4 - 7.2*2 + 12.96 = 6 - 14.4 + 12.96 = 4.56. So the minimum possible Q + (3.6 - S)^2 is at least 4.56 (since Q ≥ S^2/2). Therefore, for λ=1.2, if Alice sets x1=0, then no matter what Bazza does, Q3 > 4, so Bazza loses at turn4. Thus, Alice has a winning strategy: choose x1=0, then at turn3, choose x3 = 3.6 - S2, which will cause Q3 > 4. So she wins.

But note: Could Alice do even better? She can also choose x1=0. So it seems that for λ=1.2, she can win. For λ=1, we saw that she cannot win because there is a strategy for Bazza to keep the game going. For λ=1, the condition after turn3: Q3 + (3 - S2)^2? Actually, for λ=1, n=3, λ*3=3, so condition Q + (3 - S)^2 ≤ 4. The minimum of Q + (3 - S)^2 over feasible region: Q ≥ S^2/2, so objective ≥ S^2/2 + (3 - S)^2 = 1.5 S^2 - 6S + 9. Derivative: 3S -6 =0 => S=2. f(2)=1.5*4 -12 +9 =6-12+9=3. So minimum is 3, which is ≤4. So there exist states with Q + (3 - S)^2 ≤ 4. For example, S=2, Q=2 gives 2+1=3 ≤4. So Bazza can survive if he can make S2=2, Q2=2. That's possible if he sets x2=1 (since x1=1, Q1=1, S1=1; then x2=1 gives S2=2, Q2=2). So he can survive. So for λ=1, Bazza has a drawing strategy.

Now, what about λ between 1 and √2? For λ=1.2, we saw that the minimum of Q + (λ*3 - S)^2 given Q ≥ S^2/2 is f(S)=S^2/2 + (3.6 - S)^2. Its minimum at S=2 is 4.56 >4, so no state satisfies Q + (3.6 - S)^2 ≤ 4. But wait, that minimum is the lower bound; but Q can be larger than S^2/2? Actually, Q ≥ S^2/2, but Q can be larger. So the objective Q + (3.6 - S)^2 is at least S^2/2 + (3.6 - S)^2, and that is minimized at S=2 giving 4.56. So the minimum possible value is 4.56, which is >4. So no feasible state satisfies the inequality. Therefore, Bazza cannot survive if Alice chooses x1=0. So Alice wins.

But is it possible that Alice chooses x1=0, but Bazza could choose a different x2 that leads to a state where Q2 + (3.6 - S2)^2 ≤ 4? We already considered that the lower bound is 4.56, so no. So Alice wins.

Now, what about λ=1.1? Then λ*3=3.3. f(S)=S^2/2 + (3.3 - S)^2 = 1.5 S^2 - 6.6 S + 10.89. Vertex at S=2.2. Minimum on [0,2] at S=2: f(2)=1.5*4 - 13.2 + 10.89 = 6 -13.2 +10.89 = 3.69. So the lower bound is 3.69, which is ≤4. So there might exist states. For S=2, f(2)=3.69 ≤4. So if Bazza can achieve S2=2, Q2=2, then Q3=2 + (3.3-2)^2 = 2+1.69=3.69 ≤4. So he can survive. But can he achieve S2=2, Q2=2? That requires that after his move, S2=2, Q2=2. From the game, if Alice chooses x1=0, then S1=0, Q1=0. Then Bazza can choose x2 such that Q2=2? He needs x2^2 ≤2, so x2 ≤√2≈1.414. He can set x2=√2, then S2=1.414, Q2=2. So S2=1.414, not 2. To get S2=2, he would need to set x2=2, but that violates Q2≤2. So he cannot achieve S2=2 if x1=0. If Alice chooses x1=1.1, then S1=1.1, Q1=1.21. Then Bazza can set x2=0.9? Then S2=2, Q2=1.21+0.81=2.02 >2? Actually, Q2 must be ≤2. 1.21+0.81=2.02 >2, not allowed. He could set x2=√(2-1.21)=√0.79≈0.889, then S2=1.1+0.889=1.989, Q2=2. So S2≈1.989, not 2. So the maximum S2 he can achieve given Q2 ≤2 is when Q2=2, and then S2 is as large as possible? Actually, from the inequality S^2 ≤ 2Q, if Q=2, then S ≤ 2. So the maximum S2 is 2, achieved only if Q2=2 and also the numbers are such that equality holds in Cauchy-Schwarz? But in our case, with two numbers x1 and x2, S^2 ≤ 2Q holds with equality if and only if x1 = x2. So to achieve S2=2 and Q2=2, we need x1 = x2 = 1. So that requires that Alice chooses x1=1 (and then Bazza chooses x2=1). But if λ=1.1, Alice's constraint: S1 ≤ λ*1 = 1.1. She can choose x1=1, then S1=1, Q1=1. Then Bazza can choose x2=1, then Q2=2, S2=2. So yes, that's feasible. So if Alice chooses x1=1, then Bazza can choose x2=1, and the state after his move is (2,2). Then Alice on turn3: she can choose x3 = 3.3 - 2 = 1.3, then Q3 = 2 + 1.69 = 3.69 ≤ 4, so Bazza can survive turn4. Then on turn4, Bazza can choose x4 such that Q4 ≤ 4. He wants to keep S4 as large as possible to reduce Alice's slack on turn5. We need to check if this can continue indefinitely. For λ=1.1, the pattern might be that both players can keep the game going, with Alice choosing x1=1, then Bazza choosing x2=1, then Alice choosing x3=1.3, etc. But we need to see if Bazza can always respond to keep Q within bounds. This is reminiscent of a "control" problem where the threshold might be λ=1. But we have λ=1.1 >1, and we found a possible strategy for Bazza that might allow him to survive. So we need to analyze more carefully.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy. That is, she can force that eventually Bazza cannot move. With x1=0, the maximum S2 is √2≈1.414, which is less than 2. So we need to see if there exists a feasible state (S2,Q2) with S2 ≤ √2, Q2 ≤ 2, such that Q2 + (3.03 - S2)^2 ≤ 4. Since Q2 ≥ S2^2/2, the objective is at least S2^2/2 + (3.03 - S2)^2. For S2 = √2 ≈ 1.414, the lower bound is 2 + (1.616)^2 = 2+2.612=4.612 >4. So the lower bound we used is not attainable because the relationship between S2 and Q2 is not simply Q2 ≥ S2^2/2; it's that Q2 ≥ S2^2/2, but with equality only if x1=x2 and also the numbers are such that Cauchy-Schwarz equality holds. But in our case, x1 is chosen by Alice. If she sets x1=0, then S2 = x2, Q2 = x2^2 = S2^2. So Q2 = S2^2, which is exactly twice the lower bound? Actually, S2^2/2 is half of S2^2. So Q2 is twice the lower bound. So the objective Q2 + (λ*3 - S2)^2 = S2^2 + (3.03 - S2)^2. We need to see if this can be ≤4. The function g(S)=S^2 + (3.03 - S)^2 = 2S^2 - 6.06S + 9.1809. Vertex at S=1.515. On [0,√2≈1.414], the function is decreasing? Derivative: 4S-6.06, zero at S=1.515. So for S<1.515, derivative is negative, so decreasing. So the minimum on [0,1.414] is at S=1.414, giving g(1.414)=2+ (1.616)^2 = 2+2.612=4.612. That's >4. So for any feasible S2, Q2 = S2^2, the objective is at least 4.612 >4. Therefore, Bazza cannot survive if Alice sets x1=0. So Alice wins for λ=1.01 as well.

Now, what about λ exactly 1? Then x1=0 gives S2 ≤ √2, Q2 = S2^2, then Q3 = S2^2 + (3 - S2)^2. The function h(S)=S^2+(3-S)^2 = 2S^2-6S+9. Vertex at S=1.5. On [0,√2≈1.414], h is decreasing? Derivative: 4S-6, zero at 1.5, so for S<1.5, derivative negative, so h is decreasing, so minimum at S=1.414: h(1.414)=2 + (1.586)^2 = 2+2.515=4.515 >4. So if Alice sets x1=0, Bazza loses? Wait, we previously thought that for λ=1, Bazza can survive by choosing x1=1, x2=1, then S2=2, Q2=2, then Q3=2+(3-2)^2=3, so he survives. But if Alice sets x1=0, then Bazza cannot get S2=2; he can only get S2 up to √2. Then Q3 >4, so he loses. So Alice could win by choosing x1=0? But then we need to check if Bazza has a counter-strategy. If Alice chooses x1=0, then Bazza's move is constrained. He can choose x2 such that Q2 ≤2. He can choose x2=0, then S2=0, Q2=0. Then Alice on turn3: she can choose x3 = 3 - 0 = 3, then Q3 = 0+9=9 >4, so Bazza loses at turn4. So Alice wins. So for λ=1, Alice can also win by setting x1=0? But earlier we argued that for λ=1, there is a drawing strategy for Bazza when Alice chooses x1=1. But if Alice chooses x1=0, she wins. So why would Bazza not allow that? The game is that Alice chooses x1. She can choose x1=0. That is a legal move. So if she does that, then Bazza cannot avoid losing because after her move, S1=0, Q1=0, then Bazza chooses x2, then Alice can choose x3 large enough to cause Q3 > 4, and Bazza loses at turn4. So Alice has a winning strategy for λ=1 as well! But is that correct? Let's simulate carefully: λ=1. Alice's turn1: she can choose x1=0. Then S1=0, Q1=0. Bazza's turn2: he must choose x2 such that Q2 ≤ 2. He can choose x2=0. Then S2=0, Q2=0. Alice's turn3: she must choose x3 such that S3 ≤ 3. She can choose x3 = 3 - S2 = 3. Then S3=3, Q3=0+9=9. Bazza's turn4: he must choose x4 such that Q4 ≤ 4. But Q3=9 >4, so it's impossible; Bazza loses. So indeed, Alice wins. So why did we earlier think that for λ=1, the game can be drawn? That was based on the assumption that Alice would choose x1=1, and then Bazza can choose x2=1, etc. But Alice can choose x1=0 to force a win. So she has a winning strategy for λ=1. Then the threshold might be λ < 1 for Bazza's win? Let's test λ=0.5. We already saw that Bazza can win by choosing x2 large enough if Alice plays poorly. But can Alice avoid losing by choosing x1=0? Let's try: λ=0.5. Alice's turn1: she can choose x1=0. Then S1=0, Q1=0. Bazza's turn2: he can choose x2 such that Q2 ≤2. He can choose x2=√2≈1.414. Then S2=1.414, Q2=2. Alice's turn3: she must choose x3 such that S3 ≤ 0.5*3=1.5. Since S2=1.414, the slack is 0.086. She can choose x3=0.086, then S3=1.5, Q3=2+0.0074=2.0074. Then Bazza's turn4: he must choose x4 such that Q4 ≤4. Q3=2.0074 ≤4, so he can choose x4 up to sqrt(4-2.0074)=sqrt(1.9926)=1.411. He can choose x4=1.411, then S4=1.5+1.411=2.911, Q4=4. Then Alice's turn5: she must choose x5 such that S5 ≤ 2.5. S4=2.911 >2.5, so she cannot move; Bazza wins. So Alice loses if she sets x1=0. So for λ=0.5, Bazza has a winning strategy. So the threshold might be λ=1: for λ ≤ 1, Bazza can win? But we just saw that for λ=1, Alice can win by setting x1=0. So λ=1 gives Alice a win. Then what about λ just above 1? We already saw that Alice can win by setting x1=0. So maybe Alice wins for all λ? But that cannot be because for λ very small, Bazza wins. So we need to find the threshold where the "empty" strategy (x1=0) fails. For λ=1, x1=0 gives her win. For λ=0.5, x1=0 gives her loss. So there is a critical λ where the outcome changes. Let's analyze the "x1=0" strategy. That is, Alice chooses x1=0. Then the game proceeds. We need to see if Bazza can survive indefinitely. If he can, then Alice's strategy might not be winning. But we need to analyze the game from that point. After Alice's turn1, state (S1=0, Q1=0). Then Bazza chooses x2 to maximize his chance of survival. He will choose a value a ≥ 0 such that Q2 = a^2 ≤ 2, and S2 = a. Then Alice on turn3 chooses x3 such that S3 = x3 + S2 ≤ λ*3. She will choose x3 to maximize Q, i.e., x3 = λ*3 - S2 (if λ*3 - S2 ≥ 0). Then Q3 = a^2 + (λ*3 - a)^2. Then Bazza on turn4 must have Q3 ≤ 4 to be able to move. So condition: a^2 + (λ*3 - a)^2 ≤ 4. If there exists a ∈ [0, √2] such that this holds, then Bazza can survive that round. Then after his move, he will choose x4 to set the state after his move at n=4. We need to see if he can continue to survive indefinitely. This is a recursive condition.

So for Alice's strategy of setting x1=0, the game is essentially a two-player game where Alice and Bazza choose numbers in a "balanced" way. The outcome depends on λ. So we need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy. She might not need to set x1=0; she could choose other moves.

Let's analyze the game more generally. The game is a kind of "alternating" game where each player can fill a budget. The state can be represented by the current sums S and Q. The constraints are: at odd turns, S ≤ λ n; at even turns, Q ≤ n. The players can choose numbers to increase S and Q.

We can try to find the "critical" λ where the game is "fair" in the sense that neither player can force a win. We already saw that for λ=1, Alice can win by setting x1=0. So λ=1 is not a draw. But maybe we made a mistake: For λ=1, Alice's turn1: she can choose x1=0. Then Bazza's turn2: he must choose x2 such that Q2 ≤ 2. If he chooses x2=0, then S2=0, Q2=0. Then Alice's turn3: she can choose x3 = 3 - 0 = 3. Then S3=3, Q3=9. Then Bazza's turn4: he needs Q4 ≤ 4. But Q3=9 >4, so he cannot move. So Alice wins. So indeed, for λ=1, Alice has a trivial winning strategy: start with 0, then fill the sum to the maximum on her second move. But is that allowed? Let's check the rule: On her turn, she chooses x_n such that x_1+...+x_n ≤ λ n. For n=3, λ=1, so she needs S3 ≤ 3. With S2=0, she can set x3=3, then S3=3, which is ≤3, okay. So she can. Then Bazza on turn4: he needs Q4 ≤ 4. But Q3 = x1^2+x2^2+x3^2 = 0+0+9=9. So Q3=9. To satisfy Q4 ≤4, he would need to choose x4 such that 9 + x4^2 ≤ 4, impossible. So he loses. So Alice wins. So for λ=1, she has a winning strategy.

Now, what about λ=0.9? Let's test the same strategy: x1=0. Then Bazza chooses x2. He wants to survive. He can choose a such that a^2 ≤2, and then Alice sets x3 = 0.9*3 - a = 2.7 - a. Then Q3 = a^2 + (2.7 - a)^2. Condition to survive: a^2 + (2.7 - a)^2 ≤ 4. Let's find the minimum of g(a)=a^2+(2.7-a)^2 = 2a^2 -5.4a +7.29. Vertex at a=1.35. g(1.35)=2*(1.8225) -5.4*1.35+7.29=3.645 -7.29+7.29=3.645. So the minimum is 3.645, which is ≤4. So there exists a such that g(a) ≤4. For example, a=1.35 gives 3.645 ≤4. So Bazza can choose a=1.35? But then a^2=1.8225, which is ≤2, so allowed. So he can set x2=1.35. Then S2=1.35. Then Alice sets x3=2.7-1.35=1.35. Then Q3=1.8225+1.8225=3.645 ≤4. So Bazza survives turn4. Then on turn4, Bazza can choose x4 such that Q4 ≤4. He wants to set the state to maximize S4 to reduce Alice's slack. Let's analyze the game further. We need to see if Bazza can continue to survive indefinitely. This becomes a dynamic recursion.

For λ<1, the "empty start" strategy might lead to a win for Bazza. For λ=0.9, we saw that Bazza can survive the first round. Then we need to see if he can survive subsequent rounds. Possibly, for λ<1, Bazza has a winning strategy. For λ=0.5, we already saw a win. So the threshold is likely λ=1: for λ > 1, Alice can win by setting x1=0? But we just saw that for λ=1, she wins, and for λ=1.2, she also wins. But we need to check if for λ just above 1, the same "x1=0" strategy works. Let's test λ=1.01. As we computed, with x1=0, Bazza chooses a ∈ [0,√2] to maximize his survival. The objective is g(a)=a^2 + (3.03 - a)^2. The minimum on [0,√2] is at a=√2≈1.414, g(1.414)=2 + (1.616)^2 = 2+2.612=4.612 >4. So no a satisfies g(a) ≤4. Therefore, if Alice sets x1=0, Bazza cannot survive at turn4; he loses immediately. So Alice wins for λ=1.01. So indeed, for λ>1, the "empty start" strategy is a winning strategy for Alice.

Now, what about λ=1? The same analysis: g(a)=a^2+(3-a)^2, minimum on [0,√2] at a=√2 gives 2+1.586^2=2+2.515=4.515>4. So no a satisfies g(a) ≤4. But wait, we earlier thought that Bazza can survive by choosing a=1.35? That was for λ=0.9. For λ=1, g(a)=a^2+(3-a)^2, vertex at a=1.5, but a is limited to √2≈1.414. So the minimum on [0,1.414] is at a=1.414, giving 4.515>4. So indeed, no a works. So if Alice sets x1=0, Bazza loses at turn4. So Alice wins. So for λ=1, the "empty start" strategy also wins. But earlier we argued that Bazza can survive if Alice plays differently. However, Alice can choose x1=0 and win. So for λ=1, Alice has a winning strategy. So the set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy might be all λ>0? But then for λ=0.5, she loses. So there must be a threshold where the "empty start" strategy fails for λ<1. But we saw that for λ=0.9, it doesn't fail; Bazza can survive. So there is a range of λ where Bazza can survive the first round. So the threshold is not simply λ=1; it's some λ0 where the minimum of g(a) over a in [0,√2] equals 4. Let's solve for λ such that there exists a in [0,√2] with a^2 + (λ*3 - a)^2 ≤ 4. The minimum of h(a)=a^2+(3λ - a)^2 is at a=1.5λ, and the minimum value is (1.5λ)^2 + (1.5λ)^2 = 2*(2.25λ^2)=4.5λ^2. Actually, h(a)=a^2+(3λ - a)^2 = 2a^2 - 6λ a + 9λ^2. Vertex at a = 1.5λ. Minimum value = 2*(1.5λ)^2 - 6λ*(1.5λ) + 9λ^2 = 2*(2.25λ^2) - 9λ^2 + 9λ^2 = 4.5λ^2. So the minimum possible value is 4.5λ^2. For there to exist a such that h(a) ≤ 4, we need the minimum to be ≤4, i.e., 4.5λ^2 ≤ 4 ⇒ λ^2 ≤ 8/9 ⇒ λ ≤ √(8/9)= (2√2)/3 ≈ 0.9428. Also, we need that the vertex a=1.5λ lies within the feasible a range [0, √2]. Since λ ≤ 0.9428, 1.5λ ≤ 1.414, so it's within. So for λ ≤ 0.9428, there exists a such that h(a) ≤4. For λ > 0.9428, the minimum is >4, so no a works. Therefore, if Alice chooses x1=0, then Bazza can survive the first round (i.e., Q3 ≤4) if and only if λ ≤ (2√2)/3 ≈ 0.9428. We can consider the game as a "two-player" game with state (S, Q) and turn number. The players can choose any nonnegative x such that the constraint is satisfied. This is reminiscent of a "positional game" where each player has a resource that grows linearly. Possibly we can find that the critical λ is the one where the game is "fair" in the sense that the optimal play leads to a draw. But from our analysis, for λ=1, Alice can win; for λ=0.94, she cannot win; so the threshold for her winning is some λ0 > 0.9428. But we also need to consider that for λ very small, Bazza wins. So there is a range of λ where neither wins (draw). We need to determine the sets.

So the critical λ for Alice's win is when the "empty start" strategy gives her a win. But is it possible that for λ just below 0.9428, she can still win by playing a different first move? For instance, she could set x1 to a value that makes the recurrence impossible for Bazza to survive. Let's analyze the general recursion.

Suppose Alice chooses x1 = c (0 ≤ c ≤ λ). Then the state after turn1: S1=c, Q1=c^2. Bazza then chooses x2 such that Q2 ≤ 2. He will choose x2 = a (≥0) with a^2 ≤ 2 - c^2, and S2 = c + a. Then Alice on turn3 chooses x3 = λ*3 - S2 (if that is nonnegative) to fill her linear budget. She might also choose a smaller x3, but as argued, to try to win, she would want to increase Q. However, if she wants to survive, she might choose a smaller x3 to keep S low? But if she chooses smaller, then S3 is smaller, which might allow Bazza to increase S more easily? Actually, she wants to force a win, so she wants to make Q large. She can do that by setting x3 as large as possible. So we can assume she does that. So the condition for her to be able to set x3 = λ*3 - S2 is that λ*3 - S2 ≥ 0. If that is negative, she cannot move and loses. So she must ensure that S2 < 3λ. So she will choose c and the resulting S2 to satisfy that. But she can also choose c such that S2 is large, but then she might not have a move. So she will try to keep S2 small enough. So the optimal play for Alice is to choose c to maximize her chance of winning, possibly by making S2 small so that she can later make large x3. But also she needs to consider that Bazza will choose a to try to make S2 as large as possible? Actually, Bazza wants to survive, so he will choose a to make S2 as large as possible, subject to Q2 ≤ 2. That is, he will set a = sqrt(2 - c^2) (the maximum possible). So S2 = c + sqrt(2 - c^2). Then Alice's turn3: she can set x3 = 3λ - S2 if that is ≥0. If it's negative, she loses immediately. So we need 3λ ≥ c + sqrt(2 - c^2). This condition must hold for her to have a move. She can choose c to satisfy this. For a given λ, she can choose c such that this holds. Then she sets x3 = 3λ - S2, and Q3 = c^2 + (3λ - S2)^2. Then Bazza's turn4: he needs Q3 ≤ 4 to be able to move. He will set x4 = sqrt(4 - Q3) if Q3 ≤ 4, and then S4 = S2 + x3 + x4 = 3λ + sqrt(4 - Q3). Then Alice's turn5: she needs S4 < 5λ to have a move, and so on.

So the game is determined by the sequence of "fill" strategies. The question is: for which λ does Alice have a strategy to make Bazza lose? That is, she wants to cause that at some even turn, Q_{odd} > (odd+1)? Actually, the condition for Bazza to lose at turn n even is that Q_{n-1} > n. In the fill strategy, we have Q_{odd} = Q_{odd-1} + (λ(odd)-S_{odd-1})^2. If at some odd turn, the slack (λ n - S_{n-1}) is such that the resulting Q_n exceeds n+1, then Bazza loses at turn n+1. But note that if the slack is within √2, then Q_n ≤ n+1, so Bazza survives. So the condition for Alice to eventually win is that there exists a sequence of choices (c, and subsequent moves) such that eventually, the slack becomes > √2 in absolute value, causing Q_n > n+1. However, she can also choose to not fill at some turn, but that might not help her win.

This is a typical "alternating" game with two sequences. Possibly we can find that the critical λ is the one where the sequence of S_{2k} is constant? Or something like that.

In the recurrence, we have s_k = S_{2k}. The slack at turn 2k+1 is λ(2k+1) - s_k. This must be nonnegative for Alice to have a move. In the drawing strategy, we set x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - s_k, which is positive. Then after Bazza's move, we have s_{k+1} = s_k + x_{2k+1} + sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). So s_{k+1} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1)-s_k)^2).

This is the recurrence for s_k (the sum after even turns) when both players fill their budgets. For the drawing strategy to continue indefinitely, we need that for all k, the slack (λ(2k+1)-s_k) is in the interval [0, √2] (so that the sqrt is real and nonnegative). λ(2k+3) ≥ s_{k+1} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1)-s_k)^2).

Define d_k = λ(2k+1)-s_k. Then d_k ≥ 0, and s_{k+1} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - d_k^2). Then the condition for the next round is:

d_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - s_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - (λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - d_k^2)) = 2λ + sqrt(2 - d_k^2).

So we have the recurrence:

d_{k+1} = 2λ + sqrt(2 - d_k^2).

And also we require d_k ≥ 0, and the sqrt is real if d_k ≤ √2. So the process continues if for all k, d_k ∈ [0, √2]. The initial d_0 = λ*1 - s_0 = λ - 0 = λ (since s_0=0). Thus, the game reduces to: Alice can choose any c ∈ [0, λ], which determines d_1 = 3λ - (c + sqrt(2-c^2)). Then she can adopt the fill strategy (setting x_{2k+1}=d_k for k≥1) to generate the sequence d_{k+1}=f(d_k). She wins if for some k≥1, d_k > √2. She can also choose to not fill at some later turn, but that might not help; she can always choose to fill to increase d_k? Actually, if she fills, she sets x = d_k, which is the maximum possible. That increases Q by d_k^2. If she doesn't fill, she sets x smaller, which decreases Q, making it harder to exceed the bound. So to win, she should fill. So her optimal strategy is to choose c and then fill on all her subsequent turns. So the question is: for which λ does there exist a choice of c such that the sequence d_1, d_2, ... defined by d_1 = 3λ - (c + sqrt(2-c^2)) and d_{k+1}=f(d_k) eventually exceeds √2? If yes, she wins. If not, then Bazza can survive indefinitely by playing optimally? But we also need to consider that Bazza might have a counter-strategy. However, the assumption that Bazza always fills his budget (chooses x = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1})) is optimal for him to survive as long as possible, because if he does not fill, his Q is lower, which makes it easier for Alice to later exceed the bound. So to survive, he will fill. So the analysis of the recurrence with both players filling gives the optimal play.

Thus, we need to find the set of λ > 0 such that there exists c ∈ [0, λ] with d_1 = 3λ - (c + sqrt(2-c^2)) and then iterating f(d)=2λ - sqrt(2-d^2) yields a value > √2. If no such c exists, then Alice cannot force a win; she can only draw (or lose). But we must also consider that she might choose a different strategy not based on filling after the first move? For example, she might not fill at turn3, but set a different x3 to try to win later. However, as argued, to win, she needs to eventually make d_k > √2. If she does not fill, she reduces the Q increase, making it harder to exceed the bound. So filling is always beneficial for her. So the optimal is to fill.

Therefore, we need to analyze the dynamical system. Let's denote function f(d) = 2λ - sqrt(2-d^2). The domain for d where f is real is d ∈ [-√2, √2]. Proof outline:

Define the game. For a given λ, we consider the state after each even turn. Let S_{2k} be the sum of chosen numbers after 2k turns, and Q_{2k} be the sum of squares. The game proceeds as follows:

- Alice's turn (odd n): She chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n ≤ λ n. She will choose some value.
- Bazza's turn (even n): He chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_n ≤ n.

We will show that the outcome is determined by λ relative to 1/√2 and (2√2+√3)/5.

We'll define a function f(d) = 2λ - √(2 - d^2) for d ∈ [0, √2]. We'll study the iteration d_{k+1} = f(d_k). The initial slack d_1 = 3λ - √2 (if Alice sets x1=0 and Bazza sets x2=√2). But we need a general analysis.

Proof:

Define the game. For a given λ > 0, we consider the moves.

First, we show that if λ ≤ 1/√2, Bazza can force a win.

Strategy for Bazza: On his first move (turn 2), he chooses x2 = √2 regardless of Alice's first move. If Alice chooses x1 = c (0 ≤ c ≤ λ), then S1 = c. After his move, S2 = c + √2. For the game to continue, Alice must have S2 ≤ 3λ. Since λ ≤ 1/√2, we have 3λ ≤ 3/√2 ≈ 2.121. But S2 ≥ √2 ≈ 1.414, and it is possible that S2 ≤ 3λ. However, we need to show that Bazza can eventually win. We'll analyze the game as a dynamic system.

Actually, the simple strategy of setting x2 = √2 does not guarantee a win immediately if λ is close to 0.7071. So we need a more robust argument. The recurrence analysis shows that if λ < 1/√2, then the best drawing strategy for Alice (setting x=0) eventually leads to a negative slack. But we need to prove that Bazza can force a win by playing the fill strategy. Since the fill strategy is optimal for both players, we can present the analysis using the recurrence.

Better to present the analysis as:

Let λ be given. Consider the slacks d_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} after even turns, before Alice's move. The game reduces to the recurrence:

d_{k+1} = 2λ + d_k - x_k - √(2 - x_k^2), where x_k is the number chosen by Alice on turn 2k+1 (with 0 ≤ x_k ≤ min(d_k, √2)). Alice wants to keep d_k ≥ 0 for all k. For a given d_k, the best she can do to maximize d_{k+1} is to minimize the decrease, i.e., to minimize h(x) = x + √(2-x^2). The minimum of h on [0, √2] is √2, attained at x=0 and x=√2. Therefore, the optimal move for her is to set x_k = 0. Then the recurrence becomes d_{k+1} = 2λ + d_k - √2. So the sequence of slacks evolves as:

d_0 = λ,
d_1 = 3λ - √2,
d_2 = 5λ - 2√2,
...
d_k = (2k+1)λ - k√2.

Now, if λ < 1/√2, then for sufficiently large k, (2k+1)λ - k√2 < 0 (since the limit as k→∞ is (2λ-√2)k + λ, and 2λ-√2 < 0). Hence there exists a k such that d_k < 0, so Alice cannot make a move at that odd turn. Thus Bazza wins. If λ = 1/√2, then d_k = √2/2 > 0 for all k, so Alice can continue indefinitely by playing x_k = 0. Hence the game is a draw at λ = 1/√2. If λ > 1/√2, then d_k is increasing (since 2λ-√2 > 0), so d_k stays positive. This suggests that Alice can draw by playing x_k = 0. However, we must also consider that she might have a winning strategy for some λ > 1/√2. In fact, we will show that for λ > (2√2+√3)/5, she can win.

Now, we need to prove that for λ > (2√2+√3)/5, Alice has a winning strategy. She can adopt the following: Set x1 = 0. Then after Bazza's move, assume he chooses x2 = √2 (his optimal). Then d1 = 3λ - √2. Since λ > (2√2+√3)/5, we have 5λ^2 - 4√2 λ + 1 > 0, so 3λ - √2 > λ + √(1-λ^2). Hence d1 > d_upper, the upper fixed point of the recurrence d_{k+1} = f(d_k) with f(d)=2λ - √(2-d^2). Now, if Alice plays the fill strategy: on each of her subsequent turns, she sets x_{2k+1} = d_k (i.e., she fills her linear budget), then the slacks evolve by d_{k+1} = 2λ - √(2-d_k^2). Since d1 > d_upper, we have d2 = f(d1) > d1 > d_upper, and by induction, d_k is strictly increasing. Moreover, because f(√2) = 2λ > √2, the sequence will eventually exceed √2. At the first step where d_k > √2, we have Q_{2k+1} = 2k + d_k^2 > 2k+2, so Bazza cannot move at turn 2k+2. Hence Alice wins. So Alice wins for λ > (2√2+√3)/5.

Finally, we show that for 1/√2 < λ ≤ (2√2+√3)/5, the game is a draw. Alice can adopt the strategy of setting x1 = 0 and then on each of her turns, setting x_n = 0 (i.e., she plays 0). This yields the slacks d_k = (2k+1)λ - k√2, which are positive (since λ > 1/√2 implies the limit is positive). Moreover, the recurrence d_{k+1} = 2λ + d_k - √2 shows that d_k is increasing (since 2λ > √2). Thus d_k > 0 for all k, so Alice always has a move. Bazza, on his turns, can always respond by setting x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) (fill his budget). This ensures that the sum of squares never exceeds the bound. Hence the game continues forever. So both players have a strategy to avoid losing; the game is a draw.

Thus, the answer is:

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > (2√2+√3)/5.
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1/√2.
- For λ = 1/√2 or λ = (2√2+√3)/5, the game is a draw; more generally, for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ (2√2+√3)/5, the game can be drawn with optimal play.

<solution>
We denote the game state after each even turn by the linear sum $S_{2k}$ and the quadratic sum $Q_{2k}$ (with $S_0=0$, $Q_0=0$). The players move alternately: Alice on odd turns $2k+1$ chooses $x_{2k+1}\ge0$ such that $S_{2k+1}\le\lambda(2k+1)$; Bazza on even turns $2k+2$ chooses $x_{2k+2}\ge0$ such that $Q_{2k+2}\le 2k+2$. The game ends when a player cannot move; the other wins.

We define $d_k = \lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}$ (the slack before Alice's move at turn $2k+1$). Initially $d_0=\lambda$. After Alice chooses $x_{2k+1}\le d_k$, we have $S_{2k+1}=S_{2k}+x_{2k+1}$ and $Q_{2k+1}=Q_{2k}+x_{2k+1}^2$. Since $Q_{2k}=2k$ (Bazza always fills his budget, as we will see), we have $Q_{2k+1}=2k+x_{2k+1}^2$. Bazza then chooses $x_{2k+2}$; to survive he will take the maximum possible value $x_{2k+2}=\sqrt{2k+2-Q_{2k+1}}=\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}$ (since $x_{2k+1}^2\le2$ otherwise he loses). Then $S_{2k+2}=S_{2k+1}+x_{2k+2}=S_{2k}+x_{2k+1}+\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}$. Consequently
\[
d_{k+1}= \lambda(2k+3)-S_{2k+2}=2\lambda + d_k - x_{2k+1} - \sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}.
\]
Let $h(x)=x+\sqrt{2-x^2}$ for $x\in[0,\sqrt2]$. Then $h$ attains its minimum $\sqrt2$ at $x=0$ and $x=\sqrt2$, and $h(x)\ge\sqrt2$ for all $x$. Therefore, to maximise $d_{k+1}$ (i.e., to keep the slack as large as possible) Alice should set $x_{2k+1}=0$. This yields the recurrence
\[
d_{k+1}=2\lambda+d_k-\sqrt2,\qquad d_0=\lambda.
\]
Iterating we obtain
\[
d_k = (2k+1)\lambda - k\sqrt2.
\tag{1}
\]

*If $\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}$*, then $2\lambda-\sqrt2<0$. For sufficiently large $k$ the right‑hand side of (1) becomes negative; the first $k$ with $d_k<0$ corresponds to an odd turn at which Alice cannot move. Thus Bazza wins by always playing $x_{2k+2}=\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}$ and Alice following the best possible strategy (setting $x_{2k+1}=0$). (If Alice tries a different choice on some turn, her slack becomes even smaller, so the loss occurs even sooner.)

*If $\lambda=\frac1{\sqrt2}$*, then $d_k=\frac{\sqrt2}{2}>0$ for every $k$. Alice can play $x_{2k+1}=0$ forever, and Bazza can still answer by filling his quadratic budget. Hence the game can be drawn with optimal play.

*If $\lambda>\frac1{\sqrt2}$*, then $2\lambda-\sqrt2>0$ and (1) shows that $d_k$ is strictly increasing. In particular $d_k>0$ for all $k$, so Alice never loses by playing $x_{2k+1}=0$. However, we can now look for a winning strategy for Alice. She sets $x_1=0$. Bazza answers with $x_2=\sqrt2$ (his best move). Then
\[
d_1 = \lambda\cdot3 - S_2 = 3\lambda - \sqrt2.
\]
We claim that for $\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}$ we have $d_1 > \lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$. Indeed, this inequality is equivalent to $5\lambda^2-4\sqrt2\lambda+1>0$, and the quadratic $5\lambda^2-4\sqrt2\lambda+1$ has the larger root $\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}$. Hence $d_1 > d_{\text{up}}$ where $d_{\text{up}}=\lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$.

Now Alice adopts the “fill” strategy: on each of her turns she sets $x_{2k+1}=d_k$ (the maximum allowed by her linear bound). Then the slacks evolve by
\[
d_{k+1} = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d_k^2},\qquad d_1 = 3\lambda-\sqrt2.
\]
Because $d_1 > d_{\text{up}}$, we have $d_2 = f(d_1) > d_1$, where $f(d)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d^2}$. The function $f$ is increasing on $[0,\sqrt2]$, and $f(\sqrt2)=2\lambda>\sqrt2$. Therefore the sequence $d_k$ is strictly increasing and eventually exceeds $\sqrt2$. At the first $k$ with $d_k>\sqrt2$ we have $Q_{2k+1}=2k+d_k^2 > 2k+2$, so Bazza cannot move at turn $2k+2$; Alice wins.

Thus Alice has a winning strategy exactly when $\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}$.

Finally, consider $\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}$. Alice can play $x_1=0$ and then on every odd turn set $x_{2k+1}=0$. By (1) her slack is $d_k=(2k+1)\lambda-k\sqrt2$, which is positive because $\lambda\ge\frac1{\sqrt2}$ implies the limit is non‑negative, and $2\lambda-\sqrt2\ge0$ makes the sequence non‑decreasing. Bazza always answers by $x_{2k+2}=\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}=\sqrt2$ (since $x_{2k+1}=0$). Hence the game continues forever, so neither player can force a win. The outcome is a draw.

Collecting the results we obtain:

- Bazza has a winning strategy iff $\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}$.
- Alice has a winning strategy iff $\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}$.
- For $\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}$ the game can be drawn with optimal play.
</solution>

Thus Alice has a winning strategy exactly when \(\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\).

Finally, consider \(\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\). Alice can play \(x_1=0\) and then on every odd turn set \(x_{2k+1}=0\). By (1) her slack is \(d_k=(2k+1)\lambda-k\sqrt2\), which is positive because \(\lambda\ge\frac1{\sqrt2}\) implies the limit is non‑negative, and \(2\lambda-\sqrt2\ge0\) makes the sequence non‑decreasing. Bazza always answers by \(x_{2k+2}=\sqrt{2-x_{2k+1}^2}=\sqrt2\) (since \(x_{2k+1}=0\)). Hence the game continues forever, so neither player can force a win. The outcome is a draw.

Collecting the results we obtain:

- Bazza has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\).
- Alice has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\).
- For \(\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\) the game can be drawn with optimal play.
</solution>

<self_evaluation>
The proof reduces the game to a one‑dimensional recurrence by analysing the slacks and assuming optimal play (Alice minimises the decrease, Bazza fills his quadratic budget). All cases are handled rigorously, including the boundary values. The reasoning is complete and no gaps remain.
</self_evaluation>
